function getParameterByName(name, url) {
		if(url == null){
		  url = window.location.href;
		}
	    name = name.replace(/[\[\]]/g, '\\$&');
	    var regex = new RegExp('[?&]' + name + '(=([^&#]*)|&|#|$)'),
		results = regex.exec(url);
	    if (!results) return null;
	    if (!results[2]) return '';
	    return decodeURIComponent(results[2].replace(/\+/g, ' '));
	}

<script type="application/ld+json">{"@context":"https:\/\/schema.org","@type":"BlogPosting","headline":"Project AK47: Uncovering a Link to the SharePoint Vulnerability Attacks","name":"Project AK47: Uncovering a Link to the SharePoint Vulnerability Attacks","description":"Project AK47, a toolset including ransomware, was used to leverage SharePoint exploit chain ToolShell. This activity overlaps with Storm-2603.","url":"https:\/\/unit42.paloaltonetworks.com\/ak47-activity-linked-to-sharepoint-vulnerabilities\/","mainEntityOfPage":"https:\/\/unit42.paloaltonetworks.com\/ak47-activity-linked-to-sharepoint-vulnerabilities\/","datePublished":"August 5, 2025","articleBody":"Executive Summary\r\nUnit 42 observed notable overlaps between Microsoft\u2019s reporting on ToolShell activity (an exploit chain affecting SharePoint vulnerabilities) and activity that we have been separately tracking. The activity, which we track as CL-CRI-1040, caught our attention by deploying a tool set that we call Project AK47, which includes a backdoor, ransomware and loaders.\r\n\r\nMicrosoft's report named a suspected China-based threat actor, Storm-2603. Based on our analysis of host- and network-based artifacts, we assess with high confidence that Storm-2603 is related to the activity cluster that we track as CL-CRI-1040. We initially noted this in our threat brief covering exploitation of recent SharePoint vulnerabilities, and here further expand on our observations. (See Table 1 in the body of this article for clarification of the connection.)\r\n\r\nOur key findings are:\r\n\r\n \tCL-CRI-1040 is a cluster of financially motivated activity involving the ToolShell exploit chain\r\n \tCL-CRI-1040 involves a custom tool set called Project AK47\r\n \tProject AK47 includes:\r\n\r\n \tA backdoor nicknamed AK47C2 that supports multiple protocols\r\n \tRansomware nicknamed AK47\/X2ANYLOCK\r\n \tLoaders abusing DLL side-loading\r\n\r\n\r\n \tCL-CRI-1040 was formerly identified as activity from a LockBit 3.0 affiliate and has recently been linked to a double-extortion site operating under the name Warlock Client\r\n\r\nThis threat research article includes both findings we can confidently attribute to CL-CRI-1040 and observations that remain at lower levels of certainty.\r\n\r\nPalo Alto Networks customers are better protected from the threats discussed in this article through:\r\n\r\n \tAdvanced WildFire\r\n \tAdvanced URL Filtering and Advanced DNS Security\r\n \tCortex XDR and XSIAM\r\n\r\nFor more information about protection against the ToolShell exploit chain, please see our threat brief on active exploitation of recent SharePoint vulnerabilities.\r\n\r\nIf you think you might have been compromised or have an urgent matter, contact the Unit 42 Incident Response team.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nRelated Unit 42 Topics\r\nRansomware, CVE-2025-49704, CVE-2025-49706, CVE-2025-53770, CVE-2025-53771, SharePoint\u00a0\u00a0\u00a0\u00a0\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCL-CRI-1040\r\nCL-CRI-1040 has been active since at least March 2025. Based on overlaps in host- and network-based artifacts from the Microsoft report, we have high confidence that the CL-CRI-1040 activity cluster represents the same threat actor nicknamed Storm-2603, that Microsoft observed exploiting recent vulnerabilities in SharePoint through the ToolShell exploit chain. The recent SharePoint vulnerabilities are designated CVE-2025-49704, \u200b\u200bCVE-2025-49706, CVE-2025-53770 and CVE-2025-53771.\r\n\r\nMicrosoft assessed Storm-2603 as a China-based threat actor, as of late July, but we do not have enough direct evidence to confidently attribute CL-CRI-1040 to any nation-state or cybercriminal entity. Prior to the SharePoint ToolShell exploitation campaign, however, we had already observed malicious activity from this cluster using a tool set we call Project AK47.\r\n\r\nWe have also observed in CL-CRI-1040 deployment of an IIS backdoor that a Chinese-speaking community commonly misuses, which might be a potential connection to the Chinese nexus.\r\n\r\nRetrospective investigation of CL-CRI-1040 revealed several pieces of evidence to support our assessment of this activity cluster as financially motivated. We confirmed that CL-CRI-1040 was formerly associated with a LockBit 3.0-affiliate and has recently been operating a double-extortion data leak site known as Warlock Client Leaked Data Show. However, considering that CL-CRI-1040 activity appeared alongside espionage-motivated actors in Microsoft\u2019s report, we cannot entirely rule out the possibility of nation-state motivation or cooperation between threat actors.\r\n\r\nWhile we further describe the connections throughout this article, Figure 1 below illustrates an overview of the overlaps between Storm-2603 and CL-CRI-1040. Table 1 details how this discussion relates to the Microsoft report.\r\n\r\n[caption id=\"attachment_150148\" align=\"alignnone\" width=\"1000\"] Figure 1. An overview of indicators of compromise (IoC) overlaps between Storm-2603 and CL-CRI-1040.[\/caption]\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nResearch Origin\u00a0\r\nCluster\/Group\u00a0\r\nActivity\u00a0\r\nTools\u00a0\r\nSignificance\u00a0\r\n\r\n\r\nUnit 42\u00a0\r\nCL-CRI-1040\r\nFinancially motivated activity involving the ToolShell exploit chain\r\nProject AK47: backdoor, ransomware, loaders\u00a0\r\nBased on our analysis of host and network-based artifacts, we assess with high confidence that Storm-2603 is identical to the activity cluster that we track as CL-CRI-1040.\r\n\r\n\r\nMicrosoft\r\nStorm-2603\r\nExploiting SharePoint vulnerabilities to deploy ransomware\r\nMicrosoft \u201chas observed this threat actor deploying Warlock and Lockbit ransomware in the past\u201d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nTable 1. Microsoft\u2019s report covers the activity of several threat actors. In this article, we detail our observations of CL-CRI-1040, which we assess with high confidence represents the activity of the same threat actor as Storm-2603.\r\nProject AK47\r\nProject AK47 is a collection of malware used in CL-CRI-1040 that has likely been under development since at least March 2025. Project AK47 consists of several sub-projects, including the following:\r\n\r\n \tA multi-protocol supporting backdoor named AK47C2\r\n \tCustom ransomware named AK47 ransomware (also known as X2ANYLOCK)\r\n \tA set of other supporting tools\r\n\r\nWe named this tool set based on its common PDB (Program Database) filepath names, as shown below in Figure 2.\r\n\r\n[caption id=\"attachment_150159\" align=\"alignnone\" width=\"800\"] Figure 2. Examples of PDB filepaths of Project AK47.[\/caption]\r\n\r\nAccording to the PDB filepath, Project AK47 can be divided into two main sub-projects:\r\n\r\n \tAK47C2\r\n\r\n \tThis sub-project contains tools named dnsclient and httpclient\r\n\r\n\r\n \tAK47\r\n\r\n \tThis sub-project contains tools named writenull, encrypt, 7zdllhijacked and dll_hijacked, shown in Figure 3 below\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n[caption id=\"attachment_150170\" align=\"alignnone\" width=\"900\"] Figure 3. The structure of Project AK47.[\/caption]\r\nAK47C2\r\nAK47C2 is designed as a multi-protocol supporting backdoor. The protocols supported include DNS and HTTP, referred to as dnsclient and httpclient respectively, based on their PDBs. These two backdoor instances share the following functionality:\r\n\r\n \tCommands\r\n \tCommand and control (C2) communication request and response format\r\n \tEncryption algorithm\r\n \tXOR key\r\n\r\nThe capability of these backdoors is straightforward, supporting the following features:\r\n\r\n \tSetting sleep duration\r\n \tExecuting an arbitrary command\r\n\r\nAccording to IoCs shared by Microsoft, attackers deployed both the dnsclient and httpclient components of AK47C2 as payloads for the ToolShell exploits.\r\nDnsclient\r\nThe dnsclient has been under development since at least early March 2025. The current variant uses DNS to communicate with the C2 server, as its PDB name indicates.\r\n\r\n \tC:\\Users\\Administrator\\Desktop\\work\\tools\\ak47c2\\dnsclinet-c\\dnsclient\\x64\\Release\\dnsclient.pdb\r\n\r\nThe method of C2 communication varies depending on the date of the sample. An early stage of dnsclient that we have called version 202503 was packed using UPX. Version 202503 was likely a test build because it contains several verbose error messages and uses a private IP address as its DNS server, as noted in the code snippet shown below in Figure 4.\r\n\r\n[caption id=\"attachment_150181\" align=\"alignnone\" width=\"700\"] Figure 4. Code snippet from version 2025-03 of dnsclient showing a private IP address of 10.7.66[.]10 as its DNS server.[\/caption]&nbsp;\r\n\r\nVersion 202503 of dnsclient communicates with the C2 server by XOR-encoding JSON data, converting it into a hexadecimal string and then sending it as a subdomain of the hard-coded server at update.updatemicfosoft[.]com. The XOR key (VHBD@H) is hard-coded in the binary and is shared among other AK47C2 samples.\r\n\r\nFigure 5 below illustrates the encoding algorithm to generate subdomains on the initial C2 check-in to receive a backdoor command.\r\n\r\n[caption id=\"attachment_150192\" align=\"alignnone\" width=\"1000\"] Figure 5. The encoding algorithm of the dnsclient version 202503.[\/caption]\r\n\r\nThe response of the C2 server is contained in a DNS TXT record encoded by the same algorithm. The decoded response uses the following format in JSON:\r\n{\u201ccmd\u201d: \u201c&lt;COMMANDS_TO_EXECUTE&gt;\u201d, \u201ccmd_id\u201d: \u201c&lt;COMMAND_ID&gt;\u201d}\r\nVersion 202503 of dnsclient supports multiple arbitrary command execution but does not support sleep duration management. The command execution result is sent in the following JSON format encoded with the same algorithm:\r\n{\u201ccmd\u201d: \u201c&lt;COMMANDS_TO_EXECUTE&gt;\u201d, \u201ccmd_id\u201d: \u201c&lt;COMMAND_ID&gt;\u201d, \u201ctype\u201d: \u201cresult\u201d, \u201cfqdn\u201d: \u201c&lt;HOSTNAME&gt;\u201d, \u201cresult\u201d: \u201c&lt;EXECUTION_OUTPUT&gt;\u201d}\r\nHowever, this implementation might generate a subdomain longer than the maximum length of a DNS query (255 bytes). To avoid this, dnsclient fragments the request data and sends it in multiple queries. It prepends s to the domain name in the DNS query to indicate the query represents fragmented data.\r\n\r\nIn early April 2025, the developer updated the protocol of the dnsclient to simplify and support more reliability, which we have named version 202504. In this version, the initial request to receive a backdoor command during C2 check-in generates a slightly different DNS subdomain, as shown below in Figure 6. The notable changes are that it doesn\u2019t use JSON anymore and prepends 1 to a random five-character session key to tell the C2 server that it is a task request.\r\n\r\n[caption id=\"attachment_150203\" align=\"alignnone\" width=\"1000\"] Figure 6. The encoding algorithm of dnsclient version 202504.[\/caption]\r\n\r\nThe TXT record in the DNS response is also encoded by the same algorithm, but the decoded data differs from the version 202503 of dnsclient as follows:\r\n&lt;COMMAND_TO_EXECUTE&gt;::&lt;SESSION_KEY&gt;\r\nVersion 202504 of dnsclient verifies the session key on the client side and performs a backdoor routine based on the received command. On the response request, similar to version 202503, version 202504 fragments the execution results if the encoded data is too long and prepends s to the random session key. To finalize the message, it prepends 2 to the first substring session key and a to the second substring session key.\r\nHttpclient\r\nThe httpclient has been under development since at least late March 2025 and supports HTTP communication with the C2 server, as its PDB name indicates.\r\n\r\n \tC:\\Users\\Administrator\\Desktop\\work\\tools\\ak47c2\\httpclient-cpp\\x64\\Release\\httpclient-cpp.pdb\r\n\r\nThe encoding algorithm and XOR key are the same ones used in dnsclient version 202503, because httpclient also uses JSON to send and receive messages. The original message of the C2 check-in appears as follows:\r\n{\u201ccmd\u201d: \u201c\u201d, \u201ccmd_id\u201d: \u201c\u201d, \u201ctype\u201d: \u201ctask\u201d, \u201cfqdn\u201d: \u201c&lt;HOSTNAME&gt;\u201d}\r\nThe encoded hexadecimal string is stored in the HTTP body and sent to the C2 server using the POST method. The httpclient uses curl for network communication, as noted in the curl options (CURLOPT) shown in the code snippet in Figure 7 below.\r\n\r\n[caption id=\"attachment_150214\" align=\"alignnone\" width=\"800\"] Figure 7. Code snippet of httpclient indicating the use of curl to communicate over HTTP.[\/caption]\r\nAK47 Ransomware (Aka X2ANYLOCK Ransomware)\r\nWhile analyzing AK47C2, we found an interesting PDB, indicating possible ransomware as a sub-project of Project AK47:\r\n\r\n \tC:\\Users\\Administrator\\Desktop\\work\\tools\\ai\\ak47\\cpp\\encrypt\\encrypt\\x64\\Release\\encrypt.pdb\r\n\r\nThe use of encrypt in the PDB filepath name was not a coincidence, and our investigation revealed a ransomware written in C++ that we dubbed AK47 ransomware. However, due to the .x2anylock file extension added to encrypted files, this malware is publicly referred to as X2ANYLOCK ransomware. Although we found several reports of victims and auto-generated pages related to this ransomware, at the time of writing we had seen no technical analysis on AK47\/X2ANYLOCK ransomware.\r\n\r\nThe earliest version of AK47 ransomware was observed in early April 2025, which has a slightly different PDB, using writenull instead of encrypt in the file path name:\r\n\r\n \tC:\\Users\\Administrator\\Desktop\\work\\tools\\ai\\ak47\\writenull\\x64\\Release\\writenull.pdb\r\n\r\nThis PDB didn\u2019t implement file encryption capability, but only implemented ransom note creation. The associated sample was likely a prototype of AK47 ransomware.\r\n\r\nBased on its compilation time, a sample of the fully implemented AK47 ransomware might have been compiled a few days after the likely prototype. The capabilities of this ransomware are typical of other ransomware families. AK47 ransomware can perform the following actions:\r\n\r\n \tTerminating several applications\r\n \tEnumerating all possible logical drives and network shares\r\n \tEncrypting specific types of files using a combination of AES and RSA, while excluding specified directories and files\r\n \tDropping ransom notes (How to decrypt my data.txt or How to decrypt my data.log)\r\n\r\nTo potentially evade detection, the ransomware checks the Data Modified timestamp of specific objects. If the timestamp is on or after June 6, 2026, the ransomware terminates itself, as the code snippet in Figure 8 below shows.\r\n\r\n[caption id=\"attachment_150225\" align=\"alignnone\" width=\"800\"] Figure 8. Code snippet of AK47 ransomware showing the timestamp check routine.[\/caption]\r\n\r\nThe ransom note is embedded in the AK47 ransomware binary without encryption or encoding. Figure 9 below shows an example of the ransom note. The decrypt ID differs with each binary, but the Tox ID to communicate with the threat actor is the same across all AK47 ransomware variants.\r\n\r\n[caption id=\"attachment_150236\" align=\"alignnone\" width=\"1000\"] Figure 9. Example of a ransom note generated by AK47 ransomware.[\/caption]\r\nIs This Warlock Ransomware?\r\nAccording to the Microsoft report, Storm-2603 has previously deployed ransomware named Warlock. However, since we have not found any common indicators between AK47\/X2ANYLOCK ransomware from CL-CRI-1040 and Warlock ransomware from Microsoft's article, we cannot conclusively determine the relationship between these two ransomware families.\r\nLoaders\r\nIn addition to the AK47C2 backdoor and AK47\/X2ANYLOCK ransomware, we found other sub-projects that support executing the payload via DLL side-loading, as the following PDB shows.\r\n\r\n \tC:\\Users\\Administrator\\Desktop\\work\\tools\\ai\\ak47\\cpp\\dll_hijacked\\dll_hijacked\\x64\\Release\\dllhijacked.pdb\r\n \tC:\\Users\\Administrator\\Desktop\\work\\tools\\ai\\ak47\\cpp\\7zdllhijacked\\7zdllhijacked\\x64\\Release\\My7zdllhijacked.pdb\r\n\r\nThese loaders are designed to be loaded via a legitimate executable (7z.exe in this case) and invoke the entrypoint of the AK47 ransomware DLL, as shown below in Figure 10.\r\n\r\n[caption id=\"attachment_150247\" align=\"alignnone\" width=\"800\"] Figure 10. Entrypoint of AK47 ransomware.[\/caption]\r\nOther Tools\r\nDuring our investigation, we encountered a RAR archive named Evidencia.rar containing the following:\r\n\r\n \tA copy of the AK47C2 dnsclient\r\n \tAK47 ransomware\r\n \tSeveral hacking tools\r\n\r\nWhile the source is unknown, the directory structure (Evidencia.rar\\Directorio_Public) and included files indicate this RAR archive is possibly a package of the Public directory from a victim machine. If so, the hacking tools in this archive may be part of the arsenal for CL-CRI-1040. Table 2 below shows notable files from Evidencia.rar.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nFilename\r\nSHA256 Hash\r\nFile Description\r\n\r\n\r\nnxc.exe\r\n0f4b0d65468fe3e5c8fb4bb07ed75d4762e722a60136e377bdad7ef06d9d7c22\r\nPyPyKatz\r\n\r\n\r\nSharpHostInfo.x64.exe\r\nd6da885c90a5d1fb88d0a3f0b5d9817a82d5772d5510a0773c80ca581ce2486d\r\nSharpHostInfo\r\n\r\n\r\n7z.exe\r\ne7a7cd756dfeacbdc8caa0d431f9192cb10d62da119b138fca65276ff4ab6958\r\nA legitimate executable\r\n\r\n\r\n7z.dll\r\nabb0fa128d3a75e69b59fe0391c1158eb84a799ddb0abc55d2d6be3511ef0ea1\r\nLoader for AK47 ransomware\r\n\r\n\r\nmasscan_1.3.0.exe\r\n5cc047a9c5bb2aa6a9581942b9d2d185815aefea06296c8195ca2f18f2680b3e\r\nmasscan\r\n\r\n\r\nsd.exe\r\nf01675f9ca00da067bdb1812bf829f09ccf5658b87d3326d6fddd773df352574\r\nSharpAdidnsdump\r\n\r\n\r\nPsExec64.exe\r\nedfae1a69522f87b12c6dac3225d930e4848832e3c551ee1e7d31736bf4525ef\r\nPsExec\r\n\r\n\r\nPsExec.exe\r\n078163d5c16f64caa5a14784323fd51451b8c831c73396b967b4e35e6879937b\r\nPsExec\r\n\r\n\r\nip.exe\r\nf185c91e62ca38494d7f125492058028028769a86ed169bd2fb051e43fd9fb70\r\nA CSV file\r\n\r\n\r\nclink_x86.exe\r\n011b31d7e12a2403507a71deb33335d0e81f626d08ff68575a298edac45df4cb\r\nA legitimate executable\r\n\r\n\r\nbbb.msi\r\n3b013d5aec75bf8aab2423d0f56605c3860a8fbd4f343089a9a8813b15ecc550\r\nLockBit 3.0 ransomware dropper\r\n\r\n\r\nclink_dll_x86.dll\r\ndbf5ee8d232ebce4cd25c0574d3a1ab3aa7c9caf9709047a6790e94d810377de\r\nLoader for LockBit 3.0 ransomware\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nTable 2. Notable files from the Evidencia.rar archive.\r\n\r\nOf note, the LockBit 3.0 ransomware files in Table 2 are important evidence for our attribution.\r\nRetrospective Investigation\r\nOur investigation of CL-CRI-1040 attacks revealed evidence of previous ransomware activities, including LockBit 3.0 and Warlock Client ransomware. This evidence led us to assess with high confidence that CL-CRI-1040 is financially motivated. Figure 11 provides an overview of the activities we've attributed to CL-CRI-1040.\r\n\r\n[caption id=\"attachment_150258\" align=\"alignnone\" width=\"1000\"] Figure 11. An overview of the activities we attribute to CL-CRI-1040.[\/caption]\r\nAlleged LockBit 3.0 Affiliate\r\nDuring our investigation on the Tox ID (3DCE1C43491FC92EA7010322040B254FDD2731001C2DDC2B9E819F0C946BDC3CD251FA3B694A) from the AK47 ransomware note, we discovered a database dump file associated with LockBit 3.0 ransomware.\r\n\r\nIn May 2025, an unknown actor compromised LockBit 3.0 infrastructure and leaked a database dump of the ransomware's operations. This leaked dump file contains:\r\n\r\n \tNegotiation messages\r\n \tBitcoin wallet addresses\r\n \tAffiliated user information\r\n \tOperational details\r\n\r\nIn this LockBit 3.0 dump file a username wlteaml has the same Tox ID as used in the AK47 ransomware note. The username wlteaml was registered as a LockBit 3.0 user on April 22, 2025, as shown in Figure 12.\r\n\r\n[caption id=\"attachment_150269\" align=\"alignnone\" width=\"1000\"] Figure 12. Same Tox ID in the LockBit dumped database.[\/caption]\r\n\r\nThe database indicates that the wlteaml is the last user registered as a LockBit 3.0 affiliate before the data leak. We believe the letters in the username wlteaml might stand for warlock team LockBit and indicate a tie to Warlock Client ransomware.\r\n\r\nLet\u2019s revisit the LockBit 3.0 ransomware files contained in the above-mentioned RAR archive (Evidencia.rar).\r\n\r\nBbb.msi is a malicious installer that works as a dropper of LockBit 3.0 ransomware loader. This MSI file drops two components:\r\n\r\n \tclink_x86.exe \u2013 This is a legitimate application misused to sideload the latter malicious DLL.\r\n \tclink_dll_x86.dll \u2013 This DLL is completely different from any other sub-projects of Project AK47. It performs several known anti-analysis and anti-debugging techniques, decrypts a shellcode and runs it within a legitimate DLL (d3dl1.dll) by using the DLL hollowing technique.\r\n\r\nThe final payload executed by the in-memory shellcode is explicitly LockBit 3.0. Figure 13 shows the disassembled code of a unique entrypoint from the Lockbit 3.0 ransomware sample. This code invokes ransomware behavior, associated functions and meaningless Windows API calls, as \u200b\u200ban analysis report on LockBit 3.0 previously described.\r\n\r\n[caption id=\"attachment_150280\" align=\"alignnone\" width=\"836\"] Figure 13. Disassembled code snippet from the LockBit 3.0 ransomware sample entrypoint.[\/caption]\r\n\r\nThe timeline for this sample is unusual, because the first submission date of this sample to VirusTotal was April 16, 2025, but the associated wlteaml user registration on the LockBit 3.0 portal was April 22, 2025. While we cannot yet explain this timeline gap, the inclusion of the LockBit 3.0 instance in the same archive as Project AK47 components does not seem to be a mere coincidence.\r\nWarlock Client Leaked Data Show\r\nThe AK47 ransomware Tox ID shows another link to the Warlock ransomware group, which emerged in June 2025. The ransomware's leak site on the dark web is named Warlock Client Leaked Data Show, and it displays the same Tox ID as AK47 ransomware for negotiation with its victims.\r\n\r\nWhile the website is inaccessible as of late July, we confirmed the same Tox ID from a publicly available screenshot. However, we haven\u2019t yet observed any actual ransomware used by the threat actor behind this leak site. Therefore, we lack any evidence to determine whether the AK47 ransomware has been used by the Warlock ransomware group.\r\n\r\nOn the other hand, Microsoft mentioned that Storm-2603 has previously deployed Warlock ransomware. However, since the report shares no indicators of Warlock ransomware binaries, we cannot confirm if the Warlock mentioned by Microsoft is identical to that used by the Warlock Client Leaked Data Show.\r\nConclusion\r\nOur analysis reveals overlaps between recent ToolShell exploit activity and the activity of a cluster that we track as CL-CRI-1040. This article also covers the Project AK47 tool set in detail and describes the considerations behind our attribution. This information reveals a continuously evolving threat and a complex situation behind the attacks.\r\nPalo Alto Networks Protection and Mitigation\r\nPalo Alto Networks customers are better protected from the threats discussed above through the following products:\r\n\r\n \t\u200b\u200bTheAdvanced WildFire machine-learning models and analysis techniques have been reviewed and updated in light of the indicators shared in this research.\r\n \tAdvanced URL Filtering and Advanced DNS Security identify known domains and URLs associated with this activity as malicious.\r\n \tCortex XDR and XSIAM combine several layers of protection to prevent both known and unknown malware from causing harm to endpoints, including those mentioned in this article.\r\n\r\nFor more information about protection against the ToolShell exploit chain, please see our threat brief on active exploitation of recent SharePoint vulnerabilities.\r\n\r\nIf you think you may have been compromised or have an urgent matter, get in touch with the Unit 42 Incident Response team or call:\r\n\r\n \tNorth America: Toll Free: +1 (866) 486-4842 (866.4.UNIT42)\r\n \tUK: +44.20.3743.3660\r\n \tEurope and Middle East: +31.20.299.3130\r\n \tAsia: +65.6983.8730\r\n \tJapan: +81.50.1790.0200\r\n \tAustralia: +61.2.4062.7950\r\n \tIndia: 00080005045107\r\n\r\nPalo Alto Networks has shared these findings with our fellow Cyber Threat Alliance (CTA) members. 	function getCookie(cname) {
	 	var name = cname + "=";
  		var decodedCookie = decodeURIComponent(document.cookie);
		var ca = decodedCookie.split(';');
  		for(var i = 0; i <ca.length; i++) {
    			var c = ca[i];
    			while (c.charAt(0) == ' ') {
     				 c = c.substring(1);
    			}
    			if (c.indexOf(name) == 0) {
    				 return c.substring(name.length, c.length);
    			}
  		}
  		return "";
	}

			if(nContainer.indexOf('Unit') != -1){
                            referer = 'Unit' ;
                            sessionStorage.setItem("container","Unit");
                        }
			if(nContainer.indexOf('Ngfw') != -1){
                            referer = 'Ngfw' ;
                            sessionStorage.setItem("container","Ngfw");
                        }
			document.cookie = 'navContainer=; path=/; domain=.paloaltonetworks.com; expires=' + new Date(0).toUTCString();
		}
	}
if(referer != "Prisma" && referer != "CloudCortex" && referer != "Cortex" && referer != "Sase" && referer != "Unit" && referer != "Ngfw") {
    referer = 'Unit' ;
    sessionStorage.setItem("container","Unit");
}
function callMainSitePrismaNavHTML(){
    
    var referrer_domain = 'https://www.paloaltonetworks.com';
    sessionStorage.setItem("domain",referrer_domain);
    if(referer == 'Prisma'){
        //var menu_url = referrer_domain+'/_jcr_content/globals/cleanHeaderPrisma.prismaRenderer.html';
        var menu_url = 'https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/themes/unit42-v6/unit-nav-renderer.php?type=prisma&domain=en';
        searchResultsPagePath = referrer_domain+"/search/prismasearch";
        }
    if(referer == 'CloudCortex'){
        var menu_url = 'https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/themes/unit42-v6/unit-nav-renderer.php?type=cortexcloud&domain=en';
        searchResultsPagePath = referrer_domain+"/search/cortexcloudsearch";	
    }
    if(referer == 'Cortex'){
        //var menu_url = referrer_domain+'/_jcr_content/globals/cleanHeaderCortex.cortexRenderer.html';	
        var menu_url = 'https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/themes/unit42-v6/unit-nav-renderer.php?type=cortex&domain=en';	
        searchResultsPagePath = referrer_domain+"/search/cortexsearch";	
    }
    if(referer == 'Sase'){
        //var menu_url = referrer_domain+'/_jcr_content/globals/cleanHeaderSase.saseRenderer.html';
        var menu_url = 'https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/themes/unit42-v6/unit-nav-renderer.php?type=sase&domain=en';
        searchResultsPagePath = referrer_domain+"/search/sasesearch";
    }
    if(referer == 'Unit'){
        var menu_url = 'https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/themes/unit42-v6/unit-nav-renderer.php?type=unit42&domain=en';
        searchResultsPagePath = referrer_domain+"/content/pan/en_US/search/unit42search";
		    }
    if(referer == 'Ngfw'){
        //var menu_url = 'https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/themes/unit42-v6/ngfw-cdss-nav-renderer.php';
        var menu_url = 'https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/themes/unit42-v6/unit-nav-renderer.php?type=ngfw&domain=en'; 
        searchResultsPagePath = referrer_domain+"/search/ngfwcdsssearch";
    }
    httpGet(menu_url,'menu_html');
    document.getElementById('main-nav-menu-cont').removeAttribute("style");
}
function addStyle(styles) {            
    /* Create style document */
    var css = document.createElement('style');
    css.type = 'text/css';

    if (css.styleSheet) 
        css.styleSheet.cssText = styles;
    else 
        css.appendChild(document.createTextNode(styles));

    /* Append style to the tag name */
    document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(css);
}
function httpGet(theUrl,req_type) {
    if (window.XMLHttpRequest) { // code for IE7+, Firefox, Chrome, Opera, Safari
        xmlhttp=new XMLHttpRequest();
    } else {// code for IE6, IE5
        xmlhttp=new ActiveXObject("Microsoft.XMLHTTP");
    }
    xmlhttp.onreadystatechange=function()
    {
        if (xmlhttp.readyState==4 && xmlhttp.status==200)
        {
            if(req_type == 'menu_html'){
            var nav_text = xmlhttp.responseText.replaceAll('https://static.cloud.coveo.com/searchui/v2.9159/js/CoveoJsSearch.Lazy.min.js', '');
            nav_text = nav_text.replaceAll('src="/', 'src="'+maindomain_lang+'/');
            nav_text = nav_text.replaceAll("'/content", "'"+maindomain_lang+"/content");
                        
            document.getElementById("PAN_2021_NAV_ASYNC").innerHTML = nav_text.replaceAll('href="/', 'href="'+maindomain_lang+'/');

            var lozad_back = document.getElementsByClassName('lozad-background');
            Array.prototype.forEach.call(lozad_back, function(el) {
            // Do stuff here
            var el_back_img_path = el.getAttribute('data-background-image');
            var first_pos = el_back_img_path.indexOf("'");
            var last_pos = el_back_img_path.indexOf("'",first_pos+1);
            el_back_img_path = el_back_img_path.substring(first_pos+1,last_pos);
            el.setAttribute("data-background-image",main_site_url+el_back_img_path);
            });
            const observer_lozad = lozad('.lozad, .lozad-background'); // lazy loads elements with default selector as '.lozad'
            observer_lozad.observe();
            }
            if(req_type == 'head_inline_css'){
            addStyle(xmlhttp.responseText);
            }
        }
    }
    xmlhttp.open("GET", theUrl, true );
    xmlhttp.send();    
}    
    
if(referer == 'Prisma' || referer == 'CloudCortex' || referer == 'Cortex' || referer == 'Sase' || referer == 'Unit' || referer == 'Ngfw'){
    const article = document.querySelector('#PAN_2021_NAV_ASYNC');
    if(referer == 'Prisma'){
        article.dataset.type = 'prisma';
        $('#PAN_2021_NAV_ASYNC').removeClass('default').addClass('defaultRedesigned');
    }
    else if(referer == 'CloudCortex'){
        article.dataset.type = 'cloudcortex';
    }
    else if(referer == 'Cortex'){
        article.dataset.type = 'cortex';
    }
    else if(referer == 'Sase'){
        article.dataset.type = 'sase';
    }
    else if(referer == 'Unit'){
        article.dataset.type = 'unit';
    }
    else if(referer == 'Ngfw'){
        article.dataset.type = 'ngfw';
    }
    //set class to default
    if(referer == 'Unit' || referer == 'Ngfw'){
        $('#PAN_2021_NAV_ASYNC').removeClass('default').addClass('defaultRedesigned');
    }
    callMainSitePrismaNavHTML();
}
</script>
<!--  End: Scripts Migrated From Unit42-v5 -->

    <section class="section blog-contents"> 
      <div class="pa blog-editor">
        <div class="l-container">
          <div class="be__wrapper">
            <div class="be__contents">
              <div class="be__contents-wrapper">
                <h2><a id="post-149927-_4lt92rr5muov"></a>Executive Summary</h2>
<p>Unit 42 observed notable overlaps between Microsoft’s reporting on ToolShell activity (an exploit chain affecting SharePoint vulnerabilities) and activity that we have been separately tracking. The activity, which we track as CL-CRI-1040, caught our attention by deploying a tool set that we call Project AK47, which includes a backdoor, ransomware and loaders.</p>
<p><a href="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/2025/07/22/disrupting-active-exploitation-of-on-premises-sharepoint-vulnerabilities/" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Microsoft's report</a> named a suspected China-based threat actor, Storm-2603. Based on our analysis of host- and network-based artifacts, we assess with high confidence that Storm-2603 is related to the activity cluster that we track as CL-CRI-1040. We initially noted this in our <a href="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/microsoft-sharepoint-cve-2025-49704-cve-2025-49706-cve-2025-53770/" target="_blank" rel="noopener">threat brief covering exploitation of recent SharePoint vulnerabilities</a>, and here further expand on our observations. (See Table 1 in the body of this article for clarification of the connection.)</p>
<p>Our key findings are:</p>
<ul>
<li>CL-CRI-1040 is a cluster of financially motivated activity involving the ToolShell exploit chain</li>
<li>CL-CRI-1040 involves a custom tool set called Project AK47</li>
<li>Project AK47 includes:
<ul>
<li>A backdoor nicknamed AK47C2 that supports multiple protocols</li>
<li>Ransomware nicknamed AK47/X2ANYLOCK</li>
<li>Loaders abusing DLL side-loading</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>CL-CRI-1040 was formerly identified as activity from a LockBit 3.0 affiliate and has recently been linked to a double-extortion site operating under the name Warlock Client</li>
</ul>
<p>This threat research article includes both findings we can <a href="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/unit-42-attribution-framework/" target="_blank" rel="noopener">confidently attribute</a> to CL-CRI-1040 and observations that remain at lower levels of certainty.</p>
<p>Palo Alto Networks customers are better protected from the threats discussed in this article through:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="https://docs.paloaltonetworks.com/wildfire" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Advanced WildFire</a></li>
<li><a href="https://docs.paloaltonetworks.com/advanced-url-filtering/administration" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Advanced URL Filtering</a> and <a href="https://docs.paloaltonetworks.com/dns-security" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Advanced DNS Security</a></li>
<li><a href="https://docs-cortex.paloaltonetworks.com/p/XDR" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Cortex XDR</a> and <a href="https://docs-cortex.paloaltonetworks.com/p/XSIAM" target="_blank" rel="noopener">XSIAM</a></li>
</ul>
<p>For more information about protection against the ToolShell exploit chain, please see our <a href="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/microsoft-sharepoint-cve-2025-49704-cve-2025-49706-cve-2025-53770/" target="_blank" rel="noopener">threat brief on active exploitation of recent SharePoint vulnerabilities</a>.</p>
<p>If you think you might have been compromised or have an urgent matter, contact the <a href="https://start.paloaltonetworks.com/contact-unit42.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Unit 42 Incident Response team</a>.</p>

<h2><a id="post-149927-_ryaowtmq4sw1"></a>CL-CRI-1040</h2>
<p>CL-CRI-1040 has been active since at least March 2025. Based on overlaps in host- and network-based artifacts from the <a href="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/2025/07/22/disrupting-active-exploitation-of-on-premises-sharepoint-vulnerabilities/" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Microsoft report</a>, we have high confidence that the CL-CRI-1040 activity cluster represents the same threat actor nicknamed Storm-2603, that Microsoft observed exploiting recent vulnerabilities in SharePoint through the ToolShell exploit chain. The recent SharePoint vulnerabilities are designated <a href="https://nvd.nist.gov/vuln/detail/CVE-2025-49704" target="_blank" rel="noopener">CVE-2025-49704</a>, ​​<a href="https://nvd.nist.gov/vuln/detail/CVE-2025-49706" target="_blank" rel="noopener">CVE-2025-49706</a>, <a href="https://nvd.nist.gov/vuln/detail/CVE-2025-53770" target="_blank" rel="noopener">CVE-2025-53770</a> and <a href="https://nvd.nist.gov/vuln/detail/CVE-2025-53771" target="_blank" rel="noopener">CVE-2025-53771</a>.</p>
<p>Microsoft assessed Storm-2603 as a China-based threat actor, as of late July, but we do not have enough direct evidence to <a href="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/unit-42-attribution-framework/" target="_blank" rel="noopener">confidently attribute</a> CL-CRI-1040 to any nation-state or cybercriminal entity. Prior to the SharePoint ToolShell exploitation campaign, however, we had already observed malicious activity from this cluster using a tool set we call Project AK47.</p>
<p>We have also observed in CL-CRI-1040 deployment of an <a href="https://github.com/WBGlIl/IIS_backdoor" target="_blank" rel="noopener">IIS backdoor</a> that a Chinese-speaking community commonly misuses, which might be a potential connection to the Chinese nexus.</p>
<p>Retrospective investigation of CL-CRI-1040 revealed several pieces of evidence to support our assessment of this activity cluster as financially motivated. We confirmed that CL-CRI-1040 was formerly associated with a LockBit 3.0-affiliate and has recently been operating a double-extortion data leak site known as Warlock Client Leaked Data Show. However, considering that CL-CRI-1040 activity appeared alongside espionage-motivated actors in Microsoft’s report, we cannot entirely rule out the possibility of nation-state motivation or cooperation between threat actors.</p>
<p>While we further describe the connections throughout this article, Figure 1 below illustrates an overview of the overlaps between Storm-2603 and CL-CRI-1040. Table 1 details how this discussion relates to the Microsoft report.</p>
<figure id="attachment_150148" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-150148" style="width: 1000px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img  class="wp-image-150148 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-73902-149927-1.png" alt="Diagram illustrating the attribution of cyber tools and malware. Labels show connections between different threat groups like Linen Typhoon, Violet Typhoon, and their relationships with entities like Storm-2603 and CL-CRI-1040 to ransomware and SharePoint vulnerabilities. " width="1000" height="587" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-73902-149927-1.png 2048w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-73902-149927-1-749x440.png 749w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-73902-149927-1-1192x700.png 1192w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-73902-149927-1-768x451.png 768w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-73902-149927-1-1536x902.png 1536w" sizes="(max-width: 1000px) 100vw, 1000px" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-150148" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 1. An overview of indicators of compromise (IoC) overlaps between Storm-2603 and CL-CRI-1040.</figcaption></figure>

<p><span style="font-size: 10pt;">Table 1. Microsoft’s report covers the activity of several threat actors. In this article, we detail our observations of CL-CRI-1040, which we assess with high confidence represents the activity of the same threat actor as Storm-2603.</span></p>
<h2><a id="post-149927-_wven14kmgum2"></a>Project AK47</h2>
<p>Project AK47 is a collection of malware used in CL-CRI-1040 that has likely been under development since at least March 2025. Project AK47 consists of several sub-projects, including the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>A multi-protocol supporting backdoor named AK47C2</li>
<li>Custom ransomware named AK47 ransomware (also known as X2ANYLOCK)</li>
<li>A set of other supporting tools</li>
</ul>
<p>We named this tool set based on its common PDB (Program Database) filepath names, as shown below in Figure 2.</p>
<figure id="attachment_150159" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-150159" style="width: 800px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img  class="wp-image-150159 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-79174-149927-2.png" alt="Screenshot of four computer file paths in a list highlighting the ak47c2 portion of each patch. " width="800" height="114" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-79174-149927-2.png 1318w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-79174-149927-2-786x112.png 786w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-79174-149927-2-768x110.png 768w" sizes="(max-width: 800px) 100vw, 800px" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-150159" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 2. Examples of PDB filepaths of Project AK47.</figcaption></figure>
<p>According to the PDB filepath, Project AK47 can be divided into two main sub-projects:</p>
<ul>
<li>AK47C2
<ul>
<li>This sub-project contains tools named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">dnsclient</span> and <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">httpclient</span></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>AK47
<ul>
<li>This sub-project contains tools named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">writenull</span>, <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">encrypt</span>, <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">7zdllhijacked</span> and <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">dll_hijacked</span>, shown in Figure 3 below</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<figure id="attachment_150170" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-150170" style="width: 900px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img  class="wp-image-150170 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-83003-149927-3.png" alt="Diagram titled &quot;Project AK47&quot; showing two branches. The first branch, labeled &quot;AK47C2,&quot; includes details of a DNS-based backdoor and an HTTP-based backdoor. The second branch, labeled &quot;AK47,&quot; lists a prototype of AK47/XANLYLOCK ransomware, an encrypt tool, and a loader of AK47/XANLYLOCK ransomware." width="900" height="914" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-83003-149927-3.png 1266w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-83003-149927-3-433x440.png 433w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-83003-149927-3-689x700.png 689w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-83003-149927-3-768x780.png 768w" sizes="(max-width: 900px) 100vw, 900px" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-150170" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 3. The structure of Project AK47.</figcaption></figure>
<h3><a id="post-149927-_174p52qw2iub"></a>AK47C2</h3>
<p>AK47C2 is designed as a multi-protocol supporting backdoor. The protocols supported include DNS and HTTP, referred to as <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">dnsclient</span> and <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">httpclient</span> respectively, based on their PDBs. These two backdoor instances share the following functionality:</p>
<ul>
<li>Commands</li>
<li>Command and control (C2) communication request and response format</li>
<li>Encryption algorithm</li>
<li>XOR key</li>
</ul>
<p>The capability of these backdoors is straightforward, supporting the following features:</p>
<ul>
<li>Setting sleep duration</li>
<li>Executing an arbitrary command</li>
</ul>
<p>According to IoCs shared by Microsoft, attackers deployed both the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">dnsclient</span> and <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">httpclient</span> components of AK47C2 as payloads for the ToolShell exploits.</p>
<h4><a id="post-149927-_w9rk1yuuvk88"></a>Dnsclient</h4>
<p>The <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">dnsclient</span> has been under development since at least early March 2025. The current variant uses DNS to communicate with the C2 server, as its PDB name indicates.</p>
<ul>
<li><span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">C:\Users\Administrator\Desktop\work\tools\ak47c2\dnsclinet-c\dnsclient\x64\Release\dnsclient.pdb</span></li>
</ul>
<p>The method of C2 communication varies depending on the date of the sample. An early stage of <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">dnsclient</span> that we have called <a href="https://www.virustotal.com/gui/file/ceec1a2df81905f68c7ebe986e378fec0805aebdc13de09a4033be48ba66da8b" target="_blank" rel="noopener">version 202503</a> was packed using UPX. <a href="https://www.virustotal.com/gui/file/ceec1a2df81905f68c7ebe986e378fec0805aebdc13de09a4033be48ba66da8b" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Version 202503</a> was likely a test build because it contains several verbose error messages and uses a private IP address as its DNS server, as noted in the code snippet shown below in Figure 4.</p>
<figure id="attachment_150181" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-150181" style="width: 700px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img  class="wp-image-150181 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-86520-149927-4.png" alt="Image displaying a segment of computer code in a programming language, featuring function calls. The code includes conditional checks and error log messages related to memory allocation and DNS server IP validation." width="700" height="305" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-86520-149927-4.png 1476w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-86520-149927-4-786x343.png 786w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-86520-149927-4-768x335.png 768w" sizes="(max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-150181" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 4. Code snippet from version 2025-03 of <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">dnsclient</span> showing a private IP address of <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">10.7.66[.]10</span> as its DNS server.</figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="https://www.virustotal.com/gui/file/ceec1a2df81905f68c7ebe986e378fec0805aebdc13de09a4033be48ba66da8b" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Version 202503</a> of <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">dnsclient</span> communicates with the C2 server by XOR-encoding JSON data, converting it into a hexadecimal string and then sending it as a subdomain of the hard-coded server at <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">update.updatemicfosoft[.]com</span>. The XOR key (<span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">VHBD@H</span>) is hard-coded in the binary and is shared among other <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">AK47C2</span> samples.</p>
<p>Figure 5 below illustrates the encoding algorithm to generate subdomains on the initial C2 check-in to receive a backdoor command.</p>
<figure id="attachment_150192" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-150192" style="width: 1000px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img  class="wp-image-150192 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-90990-149927-5.png" alt="Illustration demonstrating the process of DNS exfiltration using randomized characters and the XOR operation, concluding with a conversion to hexadecimal and alignment with DNS subdomain mask length." width="1000" height="279" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-90990-149927-5.png 2048w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-90990-149927-5-786x220.png 786w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-90990-149927-5-1920x536.png 1920w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-90990-149927-5-768x215.png 768w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-90990-149927-5-1536x429.png 1536w" sizes="(max-width: 1000px) 100vw, 1000px" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-150192" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 5. The encoding algorithm of the dnsclient version 202503.</figcaption></figure>

			<div class="crayon-main" style="">
				<table class="crayon-table">
					<tr class="crayon-row">
				<td class="crayon-nums " data-settings="show">
					<div class="crayon-nums-content" style="font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;"><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-6893ecf314669111784455-1">1</div></div>
				</td>
						<td class="crayon-code"><div class="crayon-pre" style="font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important; -moz-tab-size:4; -o-tab-size:4; -webkit-tab-size:4; tab-size:4;"><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-6893ecf314669111784455-1"><span class="crayon-sy">{</span>“<span class="crayon-i">cmd</span>”<span class="crayon-sy">:</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span>“<span class="crayon-h">&lt;</span><span class="crayon-i">COMMANDS_TO_EXECUTE</span><span class="crayon-h">&gt;</span>”<span class="crayon-sy">,</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span>“<span class="crayon-i">cmd_id</span>”<span class="crayon-sy">:</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span>“<span class="crayon-h">&lt;</span><span class="crayon-i">COMMAND_ID</span><span class="crayon-h">&gt;</span>”<span class="crayon-sy">,</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span>“<span class="crayon-i">type</span>”<span class="crayon-sy">:</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span>“<span class="crayon-i">result</span>”<span class="crayon-sy">,</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span>“<span class="crayon-i">fqdn</span>”<span class="crayon-sy">:</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span>“<span class="crayon-h">&lt;</span><span class="crayon-i">HOSTNAME</span><span class="crayon-h">&gt;</span>”<span class="crayon-sy">,</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span>“<span class="crayon-i">result</span>”<span class="crayon-sy">:</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span>“<span class="crayon-h">&lt;</span><span class="crayon-i">EXECUTION_OUTPUT</span><span class="crayon-h">&gt;</span>”<span class="crayon-sy">}</span></div></div></td>
					</tr>
				</table>
			</div>
		</div>
<!-- [Format Time: 0.0001 seconds] -->
<p>However, this implementation might generate a subdomain longer than the maximum length of a DNS query (255 bytes). To avoid this, <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">dnsclient</span> fragments the request data and sends it in multiple queries. It prepends <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">s</span> to the domain name in the DNS query to indicate the query represents fragmented data.</p>
<p>In early April 2025, the developer updated the protocol of the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">dnsclient</span> to simplify and support more reliability, which we have named <a href="https://www.virustotal.com/gui/file/1eb914c09c873f0a7bcf81475ab0f6bdfaccc6b63bf7e5f2dbf19295106af192" target="_blank" rel="noopener">version 202504</a>. In this version, the initial request to receive a backdoor command during C2 check-in generates a slightly different DNS subdomain, as shown below in Figure 6. The notable changes are that it doesn’t use JSON anymore and prepends <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">1</span> to a random five-character session key to tell the C2 server that it is a task request.</p>
<figure id="attachment_150203" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-150203" style="width: 1000px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img  class="wp-image-150203 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-95436-149927-6.png" alt="Image showing a diagram explaining a process to generate a unique subdomain using random characters, XOR operations, hostname, and conversion to hexadecimal. " width="1000" height="350" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-95436-149927-6.png 1772w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-95436-149927-6-786x275.png 786w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-95436-149927-6-768x269.png 768w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-95436-149927-6-1536x537.png 1536w" sizes="(max-width: 1000px) 100vw, 1000px" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-150203" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 6. The encoding algorithm of dnsclient version 202504.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The TXT record in the DNS response is also encoded by the same algorithm, but the decoded data differs from the <a href="https://www.virustotal.com/gui/file/ceec1a2df81905f68c7ebe986e378fec0805aebdc13de09a4033be48ba66da8b" target="_blank" rel="noopener">version 202503</a> of <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">dnsclient</span> as follows:</p><!-- Crayon Syntax Highlighter v_2.7.2_beta -->

		<div id="crayon-6893ecf31466d370023967" class="crayon-syntax crayon-theme-classic crayon-font-monaco crayon-os-pc print-yes notranslate" data-settings=" minimize scroll-mouseover wrap" style=" margin-top: 12px; margin-bottom: 12px; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
		
			<div class="crayon-toolbar" data-settings=" mouseover overlay hide delay" style="font-size: 12px !important;height: 18px !important; line-height: 18px !important;"><span class="crayon-title"></span>
			<div class="crayon-tools" style="font-size: 12px !important;height: 18px !important; line-height: 18px !important;"><div class="crayon-button crayon-nums-button" title="Toggle Line Numbers"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-plain-button" title="Toggle Plain Code"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-wrap-button" title="Toggle Line Wrap"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-expand-button" title="Expand Code"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-copy-button" title="Copy"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-popup-button" title="Open Code In New Window"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div></div></div>

				</td>
						<td class="crayon-code"><div class="crayon-pre" style="font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important; -moz-tab-size:4; -o-tab-size:4; -webkit-tab-size:4; tab-size:4;"><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-6893ecf31466d370023967-1"><span class="crayon-h">&lt;</span><span class="crayon-i">COMMAND_TO_EXECUTE</span><span class="crayon-h">&gt;</span><span class="crayon-sy">:</span><span class="crayon-sy">:</span><span class="crayon-h">&lt;</span><span class="crayon-i">SESSION_KEY</span><span class="crayon-h">&gt;</span></div></div></td>
					</tr>
				</table>
			</div>
		</div>
<!-- [Format Time: 0.0000 seconds] -->
<p><a href="https://www.virustotal.com/gui/file/1eb914c09c873f0a7bcf81475ab0f6bdfaccc6b63bf7e5f2dbf19295106af192" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Version 202504</a> of <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">dnsclient</span> verifies the session key on the client side and performs a backdoor routine based on the received command. On the response request, similar to <a href="https://www.virustotal.com/gui/file/ceec1a2df81905f68c7ebe986e378fec0805aebdc13de09a4033be48ba66da8b" target="_blank" rel="noopener">version 202503</a>, version 202504 fragments the execution results if the encoded data is too long and prepends <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">s</span> to the random session key. To finalize the message, it prepends <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">2</span> to the first substring session key and <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">a</span> to the second substring session key.</p>
<h4><a id="post-149927-_psc3958yntnn"></a>Httpclient</h4>
<p>The <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">httpclient</span> has been under development since at least late March 2025 and supports HTTP communication with the C2 server, as its PDB name indicates.</p>
<ul>
<li><span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">C:\Users\Administrator\Desktop\work\tools\ak47c2\httpclient-cpp\x64\Release\httpclient-cpp.pdb</span></li>
</ul>
<p>The encoding algorithm and XOR key are the same ones used in <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">dnsclient</span> <a href="https://www.virustotal.com/gui/file/ceec1a2df81905f68c7ebe986e378fec0805aebdc13de09a4033be48ba66da8b" target="_blank" rel="noopener">version 202503</a>, because <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">httpclient</span> also uses JSON to send and receive messages. The original message of the C2 check-in appears as follows:</p><!-- Crayon Syntax Highlighter v_2.7.2_beta -->

		<div id="crayon-6893ecf314670584190540" class="crayon-syntax crayon-theme-classic crayon-font-monaco crayon-os-pc print-yes notranslate" data-settings=" minimize scroll-mouseover wrap" style=" margin-top: 12px; margin-bottom: 12px; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">

<p>The encoded hexadecimal string is stored in the HTTP body and sent to the C2 server using the POST method. The <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">httpclient</span> uses curl for network communication, as noted in the curl options (<span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">CURLOPT</span>) shown in the code snippet in Figure 7 below.</p>
<figure id="attachment_150214" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-150214" style="width: 800px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img  class="wp-image-150214 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-98861-149927-7.png" alt="A screenshot of computer code in an editor, displaying functions and commands primarily related to the curl library for handling internet protocols. Text is in shades of blue, green, and grey." width="800" height="277" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-98861-149927-7.png 2048w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-98861-149927-7-786x272.png 786w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-98861-149927-7-1920x664.png 1920w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-98861-149927-7-768x266.png 768w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-98861-149927-7-1536x531.png 1536w" sizes="(max-width: 800px) 100vw, 800px" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-150214" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 7. Code snippet of <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">httpclient</span> indicating the use of curl to communicate over HTTP.</figcaption></figure>
<h3><a id="post-149927-_v52p0xha61oe"></a>AK47 Ransomware (Aka X2ANYLOCK Ransomware)</h3>
<p>While analyzing AK47C2, we found an interesting PDB, indicating possible ransomware as a sub-project of Project AK47:</p>
<ul>
<li><span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">C:\Users\Administrator\Desktop\work\tools\ai\ak47\cpp\encrypt\encrypt\x64\Release\encrypt.pdb</span></li>
</ul>
<p>The use of <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">encrypt</span> in the PDB filepath name was not a coincidence, and our investigation revealed a ransomware written in C++ that we dubbed AK47 ransomware. However, due to the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">.x2anylock</span> file extension added to encrypted files, this malware is publicly referred to as X2ANYLOCK ransomware. Although we found several reports of victims and auto-generated pages related to this ransomware, at the time of writing we had seen no technical analysis on AK47/X2ANYLOCK ransomware.</p>
<p>The <a href="https://www.virustotal.com/gui/file/4147a1c7084357463b35071eab6f4525a94476b40336ebbf8a4e54eb9b51917f" target="_blank" rel="noopener">earliest version of AK47 ransomware</a> was observed in early April 2025, which has a slightly different PDB, using <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">writenull</span> instead of <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">encrypt</span> in the file path name:</p>
<ul>
<li><span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">C:\Users\Administrator\Desktop\work\tools\ai\ak47\writenull\x64\Release\writenull.pdb</span></li>
</ul>
<p>This PDB didn’t implement file encryption capability, but only implemented ransom note creation. The associated sample was likely a prototype of AK47 ransomware.</p>
<p>Based on its compilation time, a sample of the <a href="https://www.virustotal.com/gui/file/79bef5da8af21f97e8d4e609389c28e0646ef81a6944e329330c716e19f33c73" target="_blank" rel="noopener">fully implemented AK47 ransomware</a> might have been compiled a few days after the likely prototype. The capabilities of this ransomware are typical of other ransomware families. AK47 ransomware can perform the following actions:</p>
<ul>
<li>Terminating several applications</li>
<li>Enumerating all possible logical drives and network shares</li>
<li>Encrypting specific types of files using a combination of AES and RSA, while excluding specified directories and files</li>
<li>Dropping ransom notes (<span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">How to decrypt my data.txt</span> or <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">How to decrypt my data.log</span>)</li>
</ul>
<p>To potentially evade detection, the ransomware checks the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Data Modified</span> timestamp of <a href="#post-149927-_zcwu38eh3ceu">specific objects</a>. If the timestamp is on or after June 6, 2026, the ransomware terminates itself, as the code snippet in Figure 8 below shows.</p>
<figure id="attachment_150225" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-150225" style="width: 800px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img  class="wp-image-150225 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-104498-149927-8.png" alt="A screenshot displaying a segment of computer code in a text editor, including file paths, system time function, and conditional statements. The code involves file operations and system time checks in a programming environment." width="800" height="338" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-104498-149927-8.png 1288w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-104498-149927-8-786x332.png 786w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-104498-149927-8-768x324.png 768w" sizes="(max-width: 800px) 100vw, 800px" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-150225" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 8. Code snippet of AK47 ransomware showing the timestamp check routine.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The ransom note is embedded in the AK47 ransomware binary without encryption or encoding. Figure 9 below shows an example of the ransom note. The decrypt ID differs with each binary, but the Tox ID to communicate with the threat actor is the same across all AK47 ransomware variants.</p>
<figure id="attachment_150236" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-150236" style="width: 1000px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img  class="wp-image-150236 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-108482-149927-9.png" alt="The image shows text providing contact information, listing both a QTox ID and an Email Support address, with email hosted at Proton.me. The decryption ID is redacted. " width="1000" height="188" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-108482-149927-9.png 2048w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-108482-149927-9-786x148.png 786w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-108482-149927-9-1920x362.png 1920w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-108482-149927-9-768x145.png 768w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-108482-149927-9-1536x290.png 1536w" sizes="(max-width: 1000px) 100vw, 1000px" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-150236" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 9. Example of a ransom note generated by AK47 ransomware.</figcaption></figure>
<h4><a id="post-149927-_cdvfxqwe616g"></a>Is This Warlock Ransomware?</h4>
<p>According to the <a href="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/2025/07/22/disrupting-active-exploitation-of-on-premises-sharepoint-vulnerabilities/" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Microsoft report</a>, Storm-2603 has previously deployed ransomware named Warlock. However, since we have not found any common indicators between AK47/X2ANYLOCK ransomware from CL-CRI-1040 and Warlock ransomware from Microsoft's article, we cannot conclusively determine the relationship between these two ransomware families.</p>
<h3><a id="post-149927-_tj80wi3tr72o"></a>Loaders</h3>
<p>In addition to the AK47C2 backdoor and AK47/X2ANYLOCK ransomware, we found other sub-projects that support executing the payload via DLL side-loading, as the following PDB shows.</p>
<ul>
<li><span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">C:\Users\Administrator\Desktop\work\tools\ai\ak47\cpp\dll_hijacked\dll_hijacked\x64\Release\dllhijacked.pdb</span></li>
<li><span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">C:\Users\Administrator\Desktop\work\tools\ai\ak47\cpp\7zdllhijacked\7zdllhijacked\x64\Release\My7zdllhijacked.pdb</span></li>
</ul>
<p>These loaders are designed to be loaded via a legitimate executable (<span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">7z.exe</span> in this case) and invoke the entrypoint of the AK47 ransomware DLL, as shown below in Figure 10.</p>
<figure id="attachment_150247" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-150247" style="width: 800px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img  class="wp-image-150247 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-112177-149927-10.png" alt="Screenshot of a computer screen displaying a list of function names and memory addresses, highlighting &quot;GetModuleProp&quot; and &quot;DllEntryPoint,&quot; with the first marked as the main entry point of the malicious routine and the latter marked as the main entry." width="800" height="405" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-112177-149927-10.png 1382w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-112177-149927-10-786x398.png 786w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-112177-149927-10-768x389.png 768w" sizes="(max-width: 800px) 100vw, 800px" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-150247" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 10. Entrypoint of AK47 ransomware.</figcaption></figure>
<h3><a id="post-149927-_ugoo7246pohv"></a>Other Tools</h3>
<p>During our investigation, we encountered a <a href="https://www.virustotal.com/gui/file/035998b724044d20d583fffa393907c7fef11ad8b93b4d423ad8cb8e53f248b7" target="_blank" rel="noopener">RAR archive</a> named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Evidencia.rar</span> containing the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>A copy of the AK47C2 dnsclient</li>
<li>AK47 ransomware</li>
<li>Several hacking tools</li>
</ul>
<p>While the source is unknown, the directory structure (<span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Evidencia.rar\Directorio_Public</span>) and included files indicate this RAR archive is possibly a package of the Public directory from a victim machine. If so, the hacking tools in this archive may be part of the arsenal for CL-CRI-1040. Table 2 below shows notable files from <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Evidencia.rar</span>.</p>

<p>Of note, the LockBit 3.0 ransomware files in Table 2 are important evidence for our attribution.</p>
<h2><a id="post-149927-_3gjs5orya9ag"></a>Retrospective Investigation</h2>
<p>Our investigation of CL-CRI-1040 attacks revealed evidence of previous ransomware activities, including LockBit 3.0 and Warlock Client ransomware. This evidence led us to assess with high confidence that CL-CRI-1040 is financially motivated. Figure 11 provides an overview of the activities we've attributed to CL-CRI-1040.</p>
<figure id="attachment_150258" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-150258" style="width: 1000px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img  class="wp-image-150258 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-115572-149927-11.png" alt="Diagram illustrating the LockBit 3.0 ransomware's use by CL-CRI-1040 and Storm-2603. It shows connections and interactions, such as 'use' and 'grant access', between various elements and entities." width="1000" height="695" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-115572-149927-11.png 1470w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-115572-149927-11-633x440.png 633w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-115572-149927-11-1007x700.png 1007w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-115572-149927-11-768x534.png 768w" sizes="(max-width: 1000px) 100vw, 1000px" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-150258" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 11. An overview of the activities we attribute to CL-CRI-1040.</figcaption></figure>
<h3><a id="post-149927-_fizkhgvoeqwm"></a>Alleged LockBit 3.0 Affiliate</h3>
<p>During our investigation on the Tox ID (<span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">3DCE1C43491FC92EA7010322040B254FDD2731001C2DDC2B9E819F0C946BDC3CD251FA3B694A</span>) from the AK47 ransomware note, we discovered a <a href="https://www.virustotal.com/gui/file/4781ec443bb563c0c7b59462d5c25b4d1cfd4ad8a8a9f15aaf1381d4f677c434/detection" target="_blank" rel="noopener">database dump file</a> associated with LockBit 3.0 ransomware.</p>
<p>In May 2025, an unknown actor compromised LockBit 3.0 infrastructure and leaked a database dump of the ransomware's operations. This leaked dump file contains:</p>
<ul>
<li>Negotiation messages</li>
<li>Bitcoin wallet addresses</li>
<li>Affiliated user information</li>
<li>Operational details</li>
</ul>
<p>In this LockBit 3.0 dump file a username <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">wlteaml</span> has the same Tox ID as used in the AK47 ransomware note. The username <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">wlteaml</span> was registered as a LockBit 3.0 user on April 22, 2025, as shown in Figure 12.</p>
<figure id="attachment_150269" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-150269" style="width: 1000px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img  class="wp-image-150269 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-119207-149927-12.png" alt="Screenshot of computer code displayed in a text editor with a black background and white text. The code includes functions and variable declarations written in Python. A Tox ID is highlighted in red on the second line." width="1000" height="81" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-119207-149927-12.png 1688w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-119207-149927-12-786x63.png 786w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-119207-149927-12-768x62.png 768w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-119207-149927-12-1536x124.png 1536w" sizes="(max-width: 1000px) 100vw, 1000px" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-150269" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 12. Same Tox ID in the LockBit dumped database.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The database indicates that the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">wlteaml</span> is the last user registered as a LockBit 3.0 affiliate before the data leak. We believe the letters in the username <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">wlteaml</span> might stand for <span style="color: #ff0000;"><strong>w</strong></span>ar<span style="color: #ff0000;"><strong>l</strong></span>ock <span style="color: #ff0000;"><strong>team L</strong></span>ockBit and indicate a tie to Warlock Client ransomware.</p>
<p>Let’s revisit the LockBit 3.0 ransomware files contained in the above-mentioned RAR archive (<span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;"><a href="https://www.virustotal.com/gui/file/035998b724044d20d583fffa393907c7fef11ad8b93b4d423ad8cb8e53f248b7" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Evidencia.rar</a></span>).</p>
<p><span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Bbb.msi</span> is a malicious installer that works as a dropper of LockBit 3.0 ransomware loader. This MSI file drops two components:</p>
<ul>
<li><span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">clink_x86.exe</span> – This is a legitimate application misused to sideload the latter malicious DLL.</li>
<li><span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">clink_dll_x86.dll</span> – This DLL is completely different from any other sub-projects of Project AK47. It performs several known anti-analysis and anti-debugging techniques, decrypts a shellcode and runs it within a legitimate DLL (<span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">d3dl1.dll</span>) by using the DLL hollowing technique.</li>
</ul>
<p>The final payload executed by the in-memory shellcode is explicitly LockBit 3.0. Figure 13 shows the disassembled code of a unique entrypoint from the Lockbit 3.0 ransomware sample. This code invokes ransomware behavior, associated functions and meaningless Windows API calls, as ​​<a href="https://www.txone.com/blog/malware-analysis-lockbit-3-0/" target="_blank" rel="noopener">an analysis report on LockBit 3.0</a> previously described.</p>
<figure id="attachment_150280" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-150280" style="width: 836px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img  class="wp-image-150280 lozad"  data-src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-122410-149927-13.png" alt="A screenshot showing a portion of code in a programming environment, with numerous &quot;call&quot; statements invoking functions. The top section with a red background is the ransomeware behavior functions. The section below it with the green background shows meaningless Windows API calls. " width="836" height="1362" data-srcset="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-122410-149927-13.png 836w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-122410-149927-13-270x440.png 270w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-122410-149927-13-430x700.png 430w, https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/08/word-image-122410-149927-13-768x1251.png 768w" sizes="(max-width: 836px) 100vw, 836px" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-150280" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 13. Disassembled code snippet from the LockBit 3.0 ransomware sample entrypoint.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The timeline for this sample is unusual, because the first submission date of this sample to VirusTotal was April 16, 2025, but the associated <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">wlteaml</span> user registration on the LockBit 3.0 portal was April 22, 2025. While we cannot yet explain this timeline gap, the inclusion of the LockBit 3.0 instance in the same archive as Project AK47 components does not seem to be a mere coincidence.</p>
<h3><a id="post-149927-_542u9yrlzi6r"></a>Warlock Client Leaked Data Show</h3>
<p>The AK47 ransomware Tox ID shows another link to the Warlock ransomware group, which emerged in June 2025. The ransomware's leak site on the dark web is named Warlock Client Leaked Data Show, and it displays the same Tox ID as AK47 ransomware for negotiation with its victims.</p>
<p>While the website is inaccessible as of late July, we confirmed the same Tox ID from <a href="https://www.ransomlook.io/screenshots/warlock-elqfbcx5nofwtqfookqml7ltx2g6q6tmddys6e25vgu3al2meim6cbqdonion.png" target="_blank"  rel="wpdevart_lightbox noopener" >a publicly available screenshot</a>. However, we haven’t yet observed any actual ransomware used by the threat actor behind this leak site. Therefore, we lack any evidence to determine whether the AK47 ransomware has been used by the Warlock ransomware group.</p>
<p>On the other hand, Microsoft mentioned that Storm-2603 has previously deployed Warlock ransomware. However, since the report shares no indicators of Warlock ransomware binaries, we cannot confirm if the Warlock mentioned by Microsoft is identical to that used by the Warlock Client Leaked Data Show.</p>
<h2><a id="post-149927-_2an8ryq91inv"></a>Conclusion</h2>
<p>Our analysis reveals overlaps between recent ToolShell exploit activity and the activity of a cluster that we track as CL-CRI-1040. This article also covers the Project AK47 tool set in detail and describes the considerations behind our attribution. This information reveals a continuously evolving threat and a complex situation behind the attacks.</p>
<h3><a id="post-149927-_htthnu6ayyia"></a>Palo Alto Networks Protection and Mitigation</h3>

<li><a href="https://docs.paloaltonetworks.com/advanced-url-filtering/administration" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Advanced URL Filtering</a> and <a href="https://docs.paloaltonetworks.com/dns-security" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Advanced DNS Security</a> identify known domains and URLs associated with this activity as malicious.</li>
<li><a href="https://docs-cortex.paloaltonetworks.com/p/XDR" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Cortex XDR</a> and <a href="https://docs-cortex.paloaltonetworks.com/p/XSIAM" target="_blank" rel="noopener">XSIAM</a> combine several layers of protection to prevent both known and unknown malware from causing harm to endpoints, including those mentioned in this article.</li>
</ul>
